  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

</script>

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer16781107792885269652">كتاب «الإسلام بين الشرق والغرب»، هو نتيجة لدراسة واسعة متعددة الجوانب لأبرز الأفكار العالمية في تاريخ البشرية المعاصر. إن ظاهرة نسيان الذات التي تميز بها التاريخ الحديث للعالم الإسلامي، تضع المفكر الشرقي والغربي على السواء في موقف مماثل من هذا الكتاب. فمن خلال الدراسة المقارنة للمقدمات الأساسية والنتائج المترتبة عليها في المجالات الاجتماعية والقانونية والسياسية والثقافية و</span>
  <span id="freeText16781107792885269652" style="display:none">كتاب «الإسلام بين الشرق والغرب»، هو نتيجة لدراسة واسعة متعددة الجوانب لأبرز الأفكار العالمية في تاريخ البشرية المعاصر. إن ظاهرة نسيان الذات التي تميز بها التاريخ الحديث للعالم الإسلامي، تضع المفكر الشرقي والغربي على السواء في موقف مماثل من هذا الكتاب. فمن خلال الدراسة المقارنة للمقدمات الأساسية والنتائج المترتبة عليها في المجالات الاجتماعية والقانونية والسياسية والثقافية والنفسية، وغيرها من المجالات للأيديولوجيتين اللتين حددتا أقدار الجنس البشري على مدى القرون الأخيرة. من خلال هذه الدراسة يكشف لنا المؤلف عن أعراض المشهد المأساوي المتزايد للتنصير والإلحاد في هذا العالم. فالمسيحية كمثال لظاهرة دينية حضارية ـ أعني دينًا بمعناه الغربي معزولاً عن قانون الوحي ـ هي فكرة شاملة للإبداع والحضارة والفن والأخلاق، وبهذا حلقت المسيحية في روحانية التاريخ. أما الإلحاد الذي يستند إلى مدخل مادي ـ الاشتراكية منظوره العملي والتاريخي ـ هذا الإلحاد هو العامل المشترك للعناصر التطورية والحضارية والسياسية والطوباوية التي تُعنى بالطبيعة المادية للإنسان وتاريخه.</span>

<div id="review_147922616" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/147922616" />
    <a title="Julia Simpson-Urrutia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4914937-julia-simpson-urrutia"><img alt="Julia Simpson-Urrutia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442624816p2/4914937.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/147922616?book_show_action=true">Feb 13, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julia Simpson-Urrutia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julia Simpson-Urrutia" href="/user/show/4914937-julia-simpson-urrutia">Julia Simpson-Urrutia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780892590575" href="/book/show/825445.Islam_Between_East_and_West">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4914937-julia-simpson-urrutia?shelf=islamic-thought-and-philosophy">islamic-thought-and-philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer147922616" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15666356694328795524">This is a heady distillation of intellectual Muslim thought, demonstrating the kind of man Izetbegovic was. I once gave this book to my father (an agnostic) who said, "He's so intelligent it's scary." Islam Between East and West is a modern treatise on cultures and civilization which attempts to show how so many philosophies have failed to give human beings what they need. Izetbegovic is no coward, and he makes strong assertions: "Every culture is theistic in its essence; every civilization is a</span>
  <span id="freeText15666356694328795524" style="display:none">This is a heady distillation of intellectual Muslim thought, demonstrating the kind of man Izetbegovic was. I once gave this book to my father (an agnostic) who said, "He's so intelligent it's scary." Islam Between East and West is a modern treatise on cultures and civilization which attempts to show how so many philosophies have failed to give human beings what they need. Izetbegovic is no coward, and he makes strong assertions: "Every culture is theistic in its essence; every civilization is atheistic." By differentiating between culture and civilization, he shows the difference between critically analytical Islamic thought ( a throwback to the Golden Age of Muslim philosophers) and the Christian turning of "the human spirit in upon itself" (witness the convents and monasteries betokening the negation of worldly life). Izetbegovic argues that the Islamic ideal offers firm middle ground between Christianity, which focuses on the spirit, and Judaism, which "represents the 'this-world' tendency." In support of the latter assertion, he writes, "The Jews have never entirely accepted the idea of immortality. . . . The Kingdom of God which the Jews were predicting before Jesus' appearance was to materialize on earth, not in heaven as the Christians believed." This, then, apparently explains why the Jewish nation has tended to focus on external progress: "It seems as if they have been constantly migrating from a civilization on the wane to one on the rise."<br /><br />Izetbegovic was the farthest thing from a fundamentalist, royalist, or nomad,which in some part explains the tepid interest of the Saudi government when he approached it during the Bosnian War, when Serbs began machine gunning hordes of Caucasian Bosnian Muslims (in front of pits--the educated first) who looked (to the Arabs) more like Westerners than Muslims. He had a secular education and later got involved in activism and then politics.<br /><br />Izetbegovic does not ignore the East: the exclusive preoccupation with this world as evinced in socialism, communism, fascism, etc.with materialism showing how if religion, by itself, does not necessarily lead to progress, "science does not lead to humanism and in principle has nothing in common with culture."<br /><br />Izetbegovic defines culture as "the art of being man," while civilization is "the art of functioning, ruling, making things perfect." Both are indispensable, he says: "Civilization educates; culture enlightens."<br /><br />He explains education as something that makes human beings more capable but not freer, better, or more human. In many ways, he contends, the progress of education has made mankind less happy, if more given to longevity. He points out that man has the propensity to grow in nobility specifically when faced with adversity, yet science treats man as an animal, and that is why psychology is an accepted science in a material world. But in a scientific world, there can be no equality or brotherhood--that is only possible when we accept that man is created by God. The equality of human beings is spiritual and not a natural, physical or intellectual fact.<br /><br />Izetbegovic uses art as proof of the existence of the soul--it matters not that the artist himself should be a believer in God. His argument? Art, he offers, is a spiritual rather than material act; the flip side to this argument is that "A human being is not the sum of his different biological functions, just like a painting cannot be reduced to the quantity of paint used." This book proves that in our modern time, there have been exceedingly erudite Muslim thinkers, far from the inflexible psychosis of fundamentalism.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15666356694328795524" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2013099061" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2013099061" />
    <a title="Murtaza " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13716226-murtaza"><img alt="Murtaza " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1531516448p2/13716226.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2013099061?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Murtaza " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Murtaza " href="/user/show/13716226-murtaza">Murtaza </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780892590575" href="/book/show/825445.Islam_Between_East_and_West">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13716226-murtaza?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13716226-murtaza?shelf=best-of-2017">best-of-2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2013099061" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3574390301214540340">In the Islamic conception of the world, mankind is unique among creation for serving as the vice-regent of the divine within the material world. While all created things are by nature obedient to the divine will and thus “Muslim,” only humans have the ability to choose whether to rebel or submit themselves to the natural order. Through their possession both an inner and outer life, humans potentially serve as the vehicle through which the divine attributes are projected into the material world, </span>
  <span id="freeText3574390301214540340" style="display:none">In the Islamic conception of the world, mankind is unique among creation for serving as the vice-regent of the divine within the material world. While all created things are by nature obedient to the divine will and thus “Muslim,” only humans have the ability to choose whether to rebel or submit themselves to the natural order. Through their possession both an inner and outer life, humans potentially serve as the vehicle through which the divine attributes are projected into the material world, providing what we could describe as a moral influence upon a creation that would otherwise be deterministic and even mechanical.<br /><br />This book by Alija Ali Izetbegovic, former President of Bosnia, is a reflection on Islam as the “middle path” between living purely for the corporeal or metaphysical worlds. Whereas Christianity tries to counsel an unworldly “pure religion” and materialist ideologies like Marxism try to counsel an equally impractical “pure materialism,” Islam is unique for charting a course that melds both exterior and interior realities – both of which humans need to feel fulfilled and at peace with existence. In Islam, humans are encouraged to engage with both the material and divine worlds in equal measure. This philosophy of the religion is embodied in the very act of Islamic prayer, which combines physical activity with meditative reflection. Izetbegovic’s conception of Islam is a consistent echo of Muhammad Asad, Ibn Arabi and a long line of Islamic philosophers reaching back into early antiquity who explained the attraction and practicality of Islam. Properly conceived, Islamic practices offer simultaneously spiritual salvation and a means for ordering the world for mankind’s wellbeing, merging both spheres of existence into a healthy balance.<br /><br />Over the course of the book, Izetbegovic conducts a comparative analysis of modern materialist ideologies with religious thought. Of great interest to me was his sympathetic take on nihilistic literature, often wrongly condemned as anti-humanistic. Nihilism arose in Western thought as a consequence of the “death of God” in Western epistemology. Suddenly, man no longer had a home in a world that now seemed alien, frightening and strangely “unnatural” to a being that materialists claim is nothing more than a biological product of the earth. <br /><br />Religion was the origin of the idea that all human beings inherently have rights and are inherently equal in creation. While the ghost of the idea of inherent human dignity and value continues to animate allegedly “secular” modern ideologies, this is a contradiction that is under constant siege by capitalism, socialism and technological advance. Deprived of the warmth of the concept of inherent human value and the natural dignity and purpose afforded by belief in divine creation, the world seems a potentially bleak and terrifying place – a predicament that nihilistic writers like Camus suggested raised the possibility of suicide as a natural response. <br /><br />According to Izetbegovic, this view of mankind as essentially a stranger in the world marks nihilism as an essentially religious yearning – the religion of those disappointed in not finding the divine. Both nihilism and religion effectively recognize the existence of a soul and an essential human foreignness to the world. The difference is religion claims to have found a way out, while nihilism does not. The difference between such a view and that held by strict philosophical materialists is significant. <br /><br />Izetbegovic cites Plato in his description of the idea of the body as the “grave of the soul,” and that, “an ethical man is not afraid of death…to truly think and live means constant preparation to die.” In Islam, submission to God and an acceptance of an external locus of control regarding worldly events is the only way out of the anxiety and unsatisfied yearning of human existence. But despite this, Islam does not denigrate the world. Indeed it encourages humans to take their enjoyment from what is made lawful to them, a message embodied in the Quran and life of the Prophet Muhammad which is often wrongly attacked by critics as an expression of sensualism and an insufficient “pure religiosity” within Islam. <br /><br />In the modern world, the religious worldview has been degraded and confused. Indeed, some ardent materialists are in practice self-sacrificing metaphysicians, while some ostensibly religious people are obsessed with materialism. The family unit has been degraded in the name of society, but society itself has been degraded by corrupt elites, leaving ordinary people nowhere to turn for comfort or support. On top of this, modern epistemologies have been obsessed with the creation of worldly utopias that essentially reduce man’s capacity for both moral or immoral action, not by nurturing mankind’s conscience but instead through basic drills and penalties. This effort may achieve some success at simply controlling the masses, but it reduces humans to nothing more than cogs in a machine or animals subject to discipline, albeit animals possessing the tool of rationality.<br /><br />It seems that with developments in technology and capitalism we are fast headed towards such a degrading future for mankind, whether we are aware of where are going or not. As Aldous Huxley predicted, human beings are beginning to merge with their technologies and become something other than “humans” in the traditional sense. This was arguably predicted as far back as Ibn Arabi and is reflected upon here in light of recent technological and social developments by Izetbegovic. Whereas religion sees life as a grand drama, modern civilization sees it as the processing of a soulless machine, where human beings are nothing more than inputs and outputs. Any surviving trace of the idea that all life has a supreme meaning and inherent value is a residue of religion, whereas ideologies like Marxism accord rights only on the basis of demands (hence its silence on the suffering of animals).<br /><br />Izetbegovic concludes with the fascinating argument that the Islamic worldview of “balance” lives on in the West, specifically in the Anglo-American tradition which retains both moral and materialistic components. Nations with Catholic histories have held onto the old dichotomy between the “Kingdom of Man” and “Kingdom of Heaven”, oscillating between the two during different periods in history and never succeeding in balancing them. Within the Anglo tradition, however, we see a retention of “the middle way” that integrates both and potentially allows for both a fruitful worldly and spiritual existence. With an eventful thirty years distance since this book was published its unclear how much of this balance remains in existence, but he argues convincingly that its roots helped stimulate the development of the Anglo world. <br /><br />This was a beautiful and nourishing book that I appreciated on both a spiritual and intellectual level. It is hard to live either in a world of pure religion or a world of pure materialism. Human beings are not built for either extreme. The idea of Islam as a balanced path between the two, a philosophy embodied in its most basic acts of prayer, fasting and charity, is a comforting vision of a world in which the various components of human existence have been brought into harmony. Izetbegovic ends the book with an ode to the Islamic worldview and the idea of submission to the divine will as the solution to the existential dilemmas of modern man. It’s a beautiful message to end on.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3574390301214540340" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_868555074" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/868555074" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5875291-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565352267p2/5875291.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/868555074?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/5875291-tim">Tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780892590575" href="/book/show/825445.Islam_Between_East_and_West">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875291-tim?shelf=islam">islam</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875291-tim?shelf=islam-history">islam-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875291-tim?shelf=islam-politics">islam-politics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875291-tim?shelf=islam-western-islam">islam-western-islam</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer868555074" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7818445916383618104">One of the greatest Islamic concepts that is all but completely obscured in today’s reactionary Western media is the fundamental idea of “The Middle Way”. This idea is lost on extremists as well, who are just as reactionary in the way they view the world as some can be in the West. Past all the cultural trappings of the way Sharia has been constructed, past the use of religion as cover for underlying grievances and perceived slights, past the misunderstanding of the deeper truths for which relig</span>
  <span id="freeText7818445916383618104" style="display:none">One of the greatest Islamic concepts that is all but completely obscured in today’s reactionary Western media is the fundamental idea of “The Middle Way”. This idea is lost on extremists as well, who are just as reactionary in the way they view the world as some can be in the West. Past all the cultural trappings of the way Sharia has been constructed, past the use of religion as cover for underlying grievances and perceived slights, past the misunderstanding of the deeper truths for which religion-as-institution only serves as a symbol, “the middle way” is precisely that space where humanity exists. It’s a forgiving space, a place of freedom that allows room for error and it’s where we actually find authentic truth. <br /><br />The middle way as the path between two extremes is a profound idea. It can be seen in everything from the resolution of opposites which true unity implies to the deep desire for a “balanced” life, to ultimately allow for a more, not less, inclusive vision of reality. The more we run to any extreme, the more we run directly into what we’re trying to avoid, and away from a unified worldview. <br /><br />This has perhaps been my greatest lesson from studying Islam. The particular language of the tradition has been one in which I’ve come to see at least a glimpse of universality. I fully acknowledge that given other life circumstances, this view of universality could have just as easily evolved from another tradition. But what it’s caused me to see is that ultimately however we describe our particular philosophy of life, the semantics we use are not as important as the truth itself. <br /><br />This is more of a reflection than a review of the book, but I believe it’s consistent with the message Izetbegovic is trying to convey. Islam between East and West is one form of a middle position, which can be described as that between culture and civilization, science and religion, rational philosophy and mysticism in metaphysics. We tend towards extremes, and as Arthur Koestler and from another position the Perennialists also describe, we have evolved into civilized worldviews which eliminate a part of the whole. <br /><br />Islam is the religion of ONE, of unity. To fully understand truly Islamic concepts in our time, it might be necessary to actually remove the term “Islam” or “Muslim” from the discussion. It might definitely be necessary to completely avoid anything resembling traditional Sharia or Islamic law. Due to cultural baggage from all sides, these terms can carry connotations today which they were never meant to imply. To realize unity, we must be inclusive, not exclusive. Izetbegovic does a masterful job of incorporating the best examples from our philosophical, scientific, political, mystical and religious traditions to show that however we’re viewing life, it’s from one perspective only, and is at best a partial truth. <br /><br />He certainly has definite positions in regards to science, political philosophies such as Marxism and yes religion itself as Islam, but his approach is one of humility, which sees the best in our various traditions and acknowledges that there is no room for dogmatism in a unified position. The space where humanity resides is fluid, and is one where there must be room for error just as there must be room for incorporating all forms of wisdom – those from areas we can explain and those mystical ones that seem to allude us as well.<br /> <br />This book was written nearly thirty years ago, but is probably more timely and more needed today than it was then, even in spite of some of the dated political references. It’s a refreshing view on some deeply polarizing ideas, and I returned to it after first reading it two years ago.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7818445916383618104" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_137877163" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/137877163" />
    <a title="Scribble Orca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4402387-scribble-orca"><img alt="Scribble Orca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1393239335p2/4402387.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer11273055053589381053">All I can say, book, is that we're off to a rocky start.<br/><br/>"There are only three integral views of the world: the religious, the materialistic, and the Islamic(1)." p. 25<br/><br/>First footnote:<br/><br/>"(1)In this book, the term religion has the meaning it has in Europe - that is, faith as an esoteric experience which does not go beyond a personal relationship with God and as such expresses itself only in dogmas and rituals.   <span id="freeText11273055053589381053" style="display:none">All I can say, book, is that we're off to a rocky start.<br /><br />"There are only three integral views of the world: the religious, the materialistic, and the Islamic(1)." p. 25<br /><br />First footnote:<br /><br />"(1)In this book, the term religion has the meaning it has in Europe - that is, faith as an esoteric experience which does not go beyond a personal relationship with God and as such expresses itself only in dogmas and rituals. Accordingly, Islam cannot be classifed as a religion. Islam is more than a religion for it embraces life."<br /><br />Now, isn't that just about what every religion claims? Just wondering, book, just wondering.<br /><br />******************************************************************************************************<br /><br />There's only just so much dogma a reader can stomach in the name of even-handedness before nausea sets in. Let's just say that I've a weak belly and further reading could have been seriously injurious to my health.</span>

<div id="review_559645081" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/559645081" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/559645081?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hamza" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hamza" href="/user/show/10197091-hamza">Hamza</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780892590575" href="/book/show/825445.Islam_Between_East_and_West">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10197091-hamza?shelf=islam">islam</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10197091-hamza?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10197091-hamza?shelf=religion">religion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer559645081" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8165382390035499361">It's difficult to really write a review for this book. It deals with Islam not as a typical "religion" per se, but as a comprehensive way of life that deals with both the spiritual and societal. Mr. Izetbegović contrasts Christianity, which is purely spiritual in his view, with Marxism and other related movements, which are purely societal in his view. In this same way, he describes the difference between culture and society, the view of man as a soul vs. as a unit of society, and many other dis</span>
  <span id="freeText8165382390035499361" style="display:none">It's difficult to really write a review for this book. It deals with Islam not as a typical "religion" per se, but as a comprehensive way of life that deals with both the spiritual and societal. Mr. Izetbegović contrasts Christianity, which is purely spiritual in his view, with Marxism and other related movements, which are purely societal in his view. In this same way, he describes the difference between culture and society, the view of man as a soul vs. as a unit of society, and many other distinctions that work toward proving his thesis of sorts. Though the book can at times be difficult to follow due to the many references made and vocabulary words used, I cannot praise it enough for explaining each of the author's opinions in detail. He truly was a very intelligent man, and it's too bad we don't have more thinkers like him nowadays.</span>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText9550978188851214203" style="display:none">NOT FOR CASUAL READING. this book took me forever to get through. i'd open it, read 2 pages, and fall asleep. the language is complicated and it can get fairly dense/repetitive at times.<br /><br />but learning wise, it is a great read. alija touches on many points - the creation of man, evolution, art/architecture/music, the modern world, morality, faith, god, the list goes on. even though I feel like i'll need to come back to it later to appreciate it fully, I feel like it's given me a good insight into the application of islam in the modern world, how it is the natural solution to the malaise suffered by western individuality and society at large. <br /><br />Creation and evolution<br />Pg 10 - moral principles reduce man's efficiency<br /><br />Pg 18- animals play, but there is always a need for it<br /><br />Pg 19 - potlatch, celebrating by destruction of material objects<br /><br />Pg 20 - biology is about the phenomena of life, not their essence<br /><br />Pg 21 - the organism feeds on negative entropy from the outside. Life is a movement against the wind of mechanical laws<br /><br />Pg 28 - war years, replenishment of sexes<br /><br />Pg 30 - the tendency of the official materialism.<br /><br />Pg 31 - he eliminates questioning<br /><br />"no meditation is needed if everything is clear"<br /><br />Pg 32 - the appropriate conclusions on the organisation of society based on Darwin's idea of man from animal. Do Muslims beauracratise like this too?<br /><br />Pg 33 - look what the animals do and do the opposite, says religion<br /><br />Pg 34 - man may create an imitation of himself soon but it will only be able to do what it is programmed to do<br /><br />Pg 35 - there cannot exist a science about the soul<br /><br />Pg 37 - why are there so many handicapped people around the houses of good, who may stand and pray next to the healthy and may even be better than them, they who have nothing to prove. Equality comes from the common origin of man<br /><br />Pg 38 -education can be inhuman<br /><br />Culture and civilization<br /><br />Pg 44 - culture is all about the afterlife, even if in rejection<br /><br />Pg 45 - civilisation is technical progress<br /><br />Pg 46 - culture is self power, civilisation is power over the world<br /><br />Pg 47 - meditation to know one place in the world<br /><br />Pg 48 - learning is about science<br /><br />Pg 49 - meditation is not about intelligence<br /><br />Education is about usefulness to society<br /><br />Pg 51 - education in capitalist countries conforms to economic requirements. Generally education serves the system.<br /><br />Pg 53 - mass culture leads to spiritual uniformity. The endless supply of kitsch<br /><br />Popular culture is authentic<br /><br />Pg 55 - mass culture is of a puerile state of mind<br /><br />Pg 56 - machines help create uniformity<br /><br />Religiousness decreases as the city increases<br /><br />Pg 57 - the barracks of a big city, passive knowledge of mass media and surrounded by objects of mass production<br /><br />Religion belongs to life arts and culture, atheism belongs to method science and civilisation<br /><br />Pg 58 - the working class in countries with vastly more civilisation than culture, ie high influence of factories and technology, are least revolutionary<br /><br />Pg 59 - more and more, machines take manual work while man is tasked with managing large automatic systems<br /><br />Technocracy<br /><br />Pg 60 - no religion means no revolution<br /><br />Official institutions are neither revolutionary nor religious<br /><br />Pg 61 - more progress in last century years than last 40 centuries (Oppenheimer)<br /><br />Standards of living do not increase happiness or humanity<br /><br />Pg 62 - our planet is an ocean of delinquents<br /><br />Pg 63 - alcoholism in developed countries<br /><br />Pg 64 - porn as an escape from routine. Gambling too. Drugs too.<br /><br />Pg 65 - technology disappearing the spirit<br /><br />Men become less satisfied with the betterment of their life<br /><br />Half of hospital beds are for mental patient s<br /><br />Pg 67 - mental degradation and health problems are an expression of civilisation itself<br /><br />Pg 68 - pollution <br /><br />Pg 69 - pessimistic philosophy comes from rich nation s <br /><br />Comfort is the lease appearance but Human life in developed nations is absurd by default<br /><br />Pg 70 - serious plays emit absurdity, positivity comes from comedy and old musicals<br /><br />Poets are the sensors of mankind<br /><br />Suicide of renowned Japanese artists<br /><br />Pg 71 - nihilism and the philosophy of the absurd come from the richest posts of the world.<br /><br />Civilisations failure to solve the problem of human happiness, but don't people already know this?<br /> <br />Pg 72 - religious story of man's origin is true because the scientific one is false. <br /><br />Nihilism is a protest against God a absence<br /><br />Materialism gives life function but no purpose. World is a zoo<br /><br />Everything is futile, but don't some people express hope from this?<br /><br />Pg 73 - atheism of despair.<br /><br /> Absurdism<br /><br />Freedom sets its aims absolutely. <br /><br />Pg 74 - destroy the myth of civilisation<br /><br />The phenomenon of art<br />Pg 77 - religion and art suppose the existence of another order. <br /><br />Darwinism as a rejection of ethics<br /><br />Pg 78 - matter, life, personality as three realities, but science only deals with the first<br /><br />Pg 79 - science and art neither succeed not depend on each other<br /><br />Philosophy is closer to science than art<br /><br />Pg 80 - all of science is an almost mechanical continuation<br /> Art is a continuous new arising<br /><br />Pg 81 - science discovers art creates<br /><br />Art is more true<br /><br />Pg 82 - the failure of the analytical approach to art should tell us something<br /><br />Pg 83 - link between art and religion. Spirit, creation, mystery, sacred, transcendnence<br /><br />Pg 84 - drama is of a religious origin<br /><br />Pg 85 - early art was part of the ritual, later being separated<br /><br />Song of songs<br /><br />Science is a daughter of astronomy, art of religion<br /><br />Pg 86 - art at its best expression through religious themes<br /><br />Pg 87 - art inspired by presentiment, feeling of transcendency<br /><br />Pg 88 - when a body of governmental authority declares it's truth<br /><br />Pg 89 - education towards civilisation, recession of art, state atheism<br /><br />Pg 90 - lag of arts and philosophy in Soviet forced socialist realism<br /><br />Pg 91 - if people have no soul then why should cities have one<br /><br />Beautiful objects come from a time when we were poorer<br /><br />Pg 92 - sociology has the average man, art has the individual<br /><br />Man does not want to be systematised<br /><br />Pg 93 - the things come from America and pile up<br /><br />Philosophy and science talk about man in general<br /><br />Pg 94 - a thing described is a thing determined<br /><br />Pg 95 - passages describing unique situations<br /><br />Pg 96 - laws of art in opposition to the mechanical and the abstract<br /><br />Pg 97 - faces as central to art. <br /><br />Art deals with moral aspect of the subject<br /><br />Pg 98 - what lies at the heart of every painting is the personality in an alien world and a conflict emerging from this basic relation<br /><br /> The soul is not the psyche, it bears responsibility and dignity<br /><br />Jung's types vs an artist's created character, the former is a monster, the others are real men<br /><br />PG 99 - The Essence of art is a longing<br /><br />Inactivity full of action<br /><br />The work of art is destroyed by multiplication<br /><br />Pg 100 - when speaking about a work we actually speak about the author. It is important what the Peter painter himself is<br /><br />PG 101 - a bad man cannot be a good poet<br /><br />Kitsch<br /><br />Everything is the opposite in Civilization<br /><br />PG 102 - completely intelligible works of art do not exist<br /><br />Creation is the aim of art while the work is it's un finished symbol<br /><br />Pg 103 - style is inscrutable, function is reasonable<br /><br />Sheer aesthetic design becomes degenerate decor<br /><br />PG 104 - critique is rationalistic, but art is not of thought<br /><br />An artist is not an intellectual<br /><br />It would be easier for the average spectator to understand the message of a work than a critic<br /><br />The Bible and Quran are not theological books<br /><br />PG 105 - there is no unreligious art<br /><br />Morality <br />Pg 109 - morality isn’t functional or rational. <br />Do we think of defeated justice/unsuccessful actions as worthless? Eg carrying man out of burning building<br /><br />Pg 110 - Moral conduct is either meaningless or it has meaning and sense in the existence of God<br /><br />Pg 111 - rhetoric used by governments to show morality, even as hypocrites.<br />“Morality is not in the act, it is the desire to live righteously”<br /><br />Pg 112 - deeds judged by intentions (religion) or consequences (revolution/ideology): negation of world vs negation of man<br /><br />Pg 113 - “an action has no moral merit in itself; to learn the moral value of man, we have to look inside. Since we can’t do it directly, we pay attention to actions, but they only remain signs of inner will”<br />“A man is not what he does but what he wants”<br /><br />Pg 114/115 - “drill has no influence on the moral attitude of man” - “behaviour that does not engage our deepest will is not an upbringing, but a drill”<br />“Drill, even when used to implement the correct habits, is essentially immoral and inhuman”<br />Individualists: man’s conversion inwardly vs Positivists: outward behaviour<br />If crime is through free choice, external reeducation has little chance of success. If crime is through bad habits, offender can be drilled to remove these<br />NOT SURE I AGREE. <br />Deen? The basics of deen are drills (acts of worship). Noobs are encouraged to do them even if they dont have full yaqeen (ie even if you’re not the best muslim, you still have to give dawah, grow a beard. Even if you dont have full yaqeen you still need to pray 5x a day). <br />Actions of worship (drills) bring down the mercy of allah. You have to question whether it’s having an affect on your heart, but this isn’t something that we can accurately determine ourselves. At the end of the day the action does boil down to upbringing (essence/heart) but we can’t tell the difference it’s having on us, so we do it anyway<br />How many of our bad habits are drills? TV, excessive sports, internet use. Do we deeply desire these?<br /><br />Pg 116/117<br />Freedom is inseperable from ethics - just like how the laws of physics insert themselves into everything<br />“Reason’s (logical) analysis of morality reduces it to nature, selfishness and egoism”<br />Reason can not give moral judgement. Nothing can be proved to be “good” or “evil” using reason alone - nature doesn’t have this. Neither can values like “liberty, equality, fraternity” or teachings of Christ be derived from reason<br />Max it can go is societal morality - social discipline, again, drill.<br /><br />Pg 118/119<br />Science vs ethics/religion/art - euthanasia, artificial insemination. <br />Religion: life and death are in the hands of God<br />Ethics: degrade man to an object, leads to his manipulation and misuse<br />Art: birth and death are mysteries and should remain as such<br /><br /><br />NO LOGICAL ARGUMENT AGAINST THE ABOVE (we see this today with how ready people have become to accept euthanasia). Just 40 years ago it was the biggest taboo, now if you argue against it you’re immoral.<br /><br />Humanism vs biology, individualism vs materialism.<br />“Biology offers him progress in exchange for his soul and human dignity”<br />“Man refuses the progress which is at hand if he has to achieve it by means disgusting to him, but will he refuse it tomorrow? Will he always refuse it?”<br /><br />Pg 120/121<br />Pure vs practical reason (one can’t accept god, one has to) <br />Science conducted individually is a spiritual quest (the pursuit of knowledge, enlightenment, the joy of learning). When it is mechanised, broken into numbers, used for the state’s military or monetary gain, it becomes material and irreligious at it’s core.<br />“Through it’s innate rejection of metaphysics and its inevitable silence on ultimate questions, science contributes to the formation of atheistic opinions - maybe not with the scientist - but certainly with the public”<br /><br /><br />Pg 122<br />Religion = belief, Morality = belief transformed into action<br />“Morality was born by prohibition and has remained a prohibition until today… history of religion is full of seemingly meaningless prohibitions… Prohibition can have a rational meaning, but utility is never it’s primary aim”<br /><br />Pg 123<br />“Moral and physical evils of the world are the main driving forces that make us spiritual beings”<br /><br />Pg 124<br />“To truly think and live means constant preparation to die” - Plato<br /><br />Pg 126/127<br />Rationalistic negation of morality <br />Von Holbach: “All beings are looking for pleasure, and all of them are afraid of pain. They call good all that causes them pleasue, and evil all that causes them pain.” <br />Re consciousness “awareness of the influence which our conduct can have on the people that surround us, and remorse is the fear we feel at the thought that our conduct can make other people hate us or be angry with us”<br />“Religion is refined egoism. Seeks to transform desire for material pleasure into moral demand”<br />Eudaemonism: moral duty is what gives you personal well being, when your life is governed by reason. <br />Asceticism, celibacy, material sacrifice, sacrifice for principles - are these things that cause pleasure? They are viewed as the pinnacle of morality.<br />CONTEMPORARY BS: “Religion just exists to make you feel good about yourself and help you justify your actions.”<br />“Women and children first?” Does being honest help, why? Should look out for self interest<br /><br />Pg 128/129<br /><br />Morals are “what is useful”. Just because something is useful doesn’t make it right eg crime. “The more profitable the murder, the less chance the murder has of being arrested”<br /><br />Pg 131<br />Social behavior is promoted by interest/self preservation, moral behaviour is not. If moral behavior was (for the benefit of society materially), we would be like animals and instinctively do it.<br /><br />Pg 132<br />“Morality is only what helps the victory of the proliterat” - Lenin. Used to justify all the murder, abductions, political dissident deaths, killing of government critics, purges, etc..<br /><br />Pg 133<br />“Authentic morality is interested only in intentions… results and consequences are in God’s hands”<br /><br />Pg 134<br />All morality owes to a religion - even concepts of respecting elders, being nice to people, etc… <br />True godless society = 1984<br /><br />Pg 135<br />“Chinese Cultural Revolution” - Mao recognised that China’s cultural/spiritual inheritance was the biggest blockade to the material progress socialism needed. <br /><br />Pg 137<br /><br />Classic atheist argument: “If I, as a free human being, hear an inner voice, without anybody’s command, which tells me not to steal or kill… If I do not derive it from any kind of absolute, social or divine, then that is not a degradation of man.”<br />Translated: I am better than a religious person. A religious man needs a book to tell him what to believe, while I am le enlightened and know what’s right or wrong my myself.<br />Why this is BS: <br />Aren’t you making yourself your God? Whatever is good is what feels right to you. Isn’t this just another, lower form of religion?<br />Marx “hope in the abstract humanity of man is no less an illusion than the pure religious illusion”<br />Engels: “Every class and every profession has it’s own morality, and they break it whenever they can and go unpunished. The love which should unite everything manifests itself in wars, conflicts, family quarrels, divorces, and exploitation of other people”<br />I.e if everyone thought like this, the world would be a massive free for all. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />Culture and History<br />Initial Humanism<br />“The present is always something more than the past and something less than the future”<br />Materialists: history = material progress<br />Spiritual: history = cultural progress, continual answering of eternal questions<br /><br />“I have seen human nature in all it’s aspects. It is the same everywhere, only the more wild it is, the more virtue is in it”<br />Makes sense if “wild” means technologically lacking<br />Yes, cultures of the east had virtue before. Does this mean western culture has no virtue? White people aren’t that bad??<br />Does this mean the routine in our lives? Does routine and comfort kill our virtue?<br />Quotes examples of American indian/African nations, how they marry, society structured, treat guests, etc… the civilized spanish slaughtered the native indians they so admired<br /><br />Art and Science<br />“In art, there is no accumulation of knowledge or experience as there is in science”<br />african/native american/islander art alongside european, even though these are underdeveloped regions<br />No further exprressive power with time<br />Questions of art are universal, science are temporal “in light of modern science, not one sentence written in these books [old science books by Aristotle] is true today”<br />“Scientists belong to their time only, poets to all times”<br /><br />Ethics and History<br />“Culture is all in the search, going back, starting anew”<br /><br />“Moral truths are constant”...”not influenced by time, place, or social circumstances”<br />Moral relativism: how does it line up with this? According to Alija most examples proving it always focus on formal behaviour vs the actual big moral.<br />Speak truth, don’t get angry, don’t hurt people, live simply, defend yours and others rights, earn your own bread, be friends with people, don’t cheat, etc… <br />Golden Rule/Categorical Imperative<br /><br />Artist and Experience<br />Picasso: 70 years of painting, 5 styles. None of them necessarily better than the other. Art is style and expression. Isn’t this that postmodern bs?<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />Drama and Utopia<br />Ideal Society<br />Drama vs utopia: “man is evil vs the social environment is evil” - man has choice vs man is a bunch of chemical reactions<br /><br />Chinese Cultural Revolution - theater nearly completely died: no drama showcasing family or personal problems. Everything was perfect<br /><br />“There are no human or moral problems in utopia”<br />The aspiration of the modern world is utopia (even ours) - buy a house, live comfortably, have a nice stable job, couple kids, die<br />In the interest of society: social discipline, abolition of family relationships (nuclear family), art at service of state (news/mass media), education, equality of sexes in division of labour, uniformity, collectivism, censorship (ironically)<br /><br />Moral dilenmas are at the core of human existence<br /><br />Utopia and Morals<br />“Man is a social animal”<br />Very ineffective one, if he were meant to be, the mechanisms would be instinctive ie ants, bees, termites<br /><br />“There are no morals in communism” - Marxist assertion<br />It’s all an instinct, nobody willingly behaves morally, its inbuilt, instinctive<br />Spider doesnt think about whether it’s being moral when it eats its siblings after hatching, bees dont think bout it when they throw older bees out of the hive<br /><br />Quoting examples of animals in moral discussions is dumb. If they had a brain, bees would eat the nectar other workers gathered themselves.<br /><br />Dependents and Heretics<br /><br />Society and Community<br />Society = based on material needs, i need to buy this from you, you give me this, etc. Community = spiritual needs.<br /><br />Personality and the Social Individual<br />“[religion] is a passion and obligation, not a comfort or a way of better living”<br /><br />“Elimination of everything specifically human, particularly individuality and freedom, seems to be the basic condition for utopia”<br /><br />Utopia and Family<br />“Social principle carried… to a state of utopia, does not recognise the family anymore”<br />What function does the family serve besides rearing the child? The emotions and attachment do nothing for the state in helping to make this utopia<br />Having kids is a form of production<br />One part of society is dedicated to producing goods, while the other can be dedicated to producing consumers<br /><br />Engels: “the single family ceases to be the economic unit of society. Private housekeeping is transformed into a social industry.”<br />Daycare, school, aged care<br /><br />“Until the myth of the family, the myth of motherhood and maternal instinct is destroyed, women will continue to be subjugated”<br /><br />Footnote on this page is great<br /><br />“Family as man’s nest, mother as the first and irreplacable teacher” vs “utopias have alw</span>

<div id="review_3325447048" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3325447048" />
    <a title="Choudhry Shuaib" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/84096717-choudhry-shuaib"><img alt="Choudhry Shuaib" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3325447048?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Choudhry Shuaib" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Choudhry Shuaib" href="/user/show/84096717-choudhry-shuaib">Choudhry Shuaib</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780892590575" href="/book/show/825445.Islam_Between_East_and_West">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3325447048" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2749619170456055402">A real diamond of a book, perhaps my favourite ever, such is the eloquence, depth and breadth of this treasure. He covers the creative heights of fiction and art to the genius of the logicians and philosophers, but they all come unstuck in the inconsistency present in their own worldview, except perhaps Nietzsche who took the ideas which form the bed rock of most modern western nations and showed their ultimate logical conclusion in nihilism. Many times I have personally heard that Islam is a re</span>
  <span id="freeText2749619170456055402" style="display:none">A real diamond of a book, perhaps my favourite ever, such is the eloquence, depth and breadth of this treasure. He covers the creative heights of fiction and art to the genius of the logicians and philosophers, but they all come unstuck in the inconsistency present in their own worldview, except perhaps Nietzsche who took the ideas which form the bed rock of most modern western nations and showed their ultimate logical conclusion in nihilism. Many times I have personally heard that Islam is a religion of the middle path or the religion of moderation and it is something I could definitely feel but could not quite convey it to people. No one has illustrated it in as beautiful a manner that Alija Izetbegovic does by showing that man himself is the one that sits at middle of endless dichotomies: religion and science, spirituality and materialism, spirit and matter , subject and object, concrete-unique and abstract-general and many more. It's only Islam and it's ideals and duties, congruent to man, sitting at the middle of these bottomless contradictory dichotomies. <br /><br />The author is aiming to provide a perspective of an Islamic outlook of the world. He starts with the premise that every philosophy can be reduced to two systems, the religious or the materialistic. Except one, Islam, which is a synthesis of both, thus ideally suited for man. The table he provides at the end of book is a superb rendering of this dualism. Islam means the call to create a man harmonious in his soul and body. Islam is a permanent searching for a state of inward and outward balance. A way of life that is a moderation between these two perspectives on life. Man is inherently inconsistent, never solely logically consistent, and so one needs a dualistic philosophy which takes this nature of man into account. Islam provides this. Another way to see this is through Godel's incompleteness theorems and it's consequences for humans and machines which are in essence abstract logical systems. Man is not mechanistic in this sense and that's because he has a spirit and this is what provides him with a creativity and hence intelligence beyond a mechanistic conception of these notions. I feel this is what Alija is trying to get across as the core theme of his message. Islam provides the only logically consistent way of life which takes the inherent nature of man into account, the materialist and the spiritual. Civilisation is the material exchange between man and nature, culture the spiritual exchange. These recurring dualisms are antagonisms and man's dualism show that man is a being of discord. <br /><br />The perspicacious author provides a sharp critique of education or technical education like science which is much more concerned with learning and applying but rarely contemplating. It's a perceptive look at the modern scientific method. "Science discovers, art creates" is a truly exceptional phrase and shines a light on the façade of science; the fact that it does not encourage creativity but in fact encourages conformity. Only once enough evidence, disproving current dogmas, has accumulated do geniuses, who think outside the current mode, get accepted and change the whole lens with which people view the world. Thomas Kuhn in his seminal book The Structure of Scientific revolutions provides much more detail then what I have given a taste of here. But this highlights the link to creativity within science and Kuhn's paradigm shifts. Science is focussed so much on logic and thus working within the current paradigm, creativity is lost and scientific progress stagnates once the revolution has passed. Scientific revolutions and paradigm shifts only come about through contemplative thinking, when one critically thinks about the systems in which they are working in/with. One needs both for the progress of society and man. <br /><br />Creativity is a purely human quality and only humans create in a unique fashion, and this comes about because of the core human essence of the soul and the inherent freedom it was endowed with. This freedom allows us to choose between what's right and wrong and is the basis of free will. And the uncertainty that comes about from this free and unique choice gives colour to the world and diversity in life, which link beautifully with the ideas of The Grammatical Man, by Jeremy Campbell. Architecture, uniquely perhaps, is a balance between creativity and functionality and as such is the middle ground between the two. Thus it is harmonious to an Islamic ideal of art and perhaps partly explains why the zenith of Islamic art or cultural expression has been architecture. This marriage between the functional and the beautiful typifies Islam, combining the mathematics of geometry with the richness of colour. It also partly explains my fascination with architecture combining my yearning for mathematical expression with my need for human creative expression. <br /><br />The first two-thirds of the book I would recommend to anyone who is interested in learning about good critiques of some very popular philosophical systems and mainly deals with the duality of world views that religion and materialism provide and how they manifest themselves when you take their foundational conceptions to their ultimate logical aim. This book goes right to the core of what it means to be human by exploring the very human notions of morality, law, art, ethics and history to probe social, psychological, political and cultural questions in a comparative and dialectical manner resulting in a comprehensive study of philosophies and world views. And then the final third tackles those very questions, which contemporary thought systems came undone by, and shows how Islam provides a way out of those internal conflicts that beset man. It's an incredibly stimulating book, from a spiritual and an intellectual perspective and one I'm sure to come back to many times over for the rest of my life, because for me this book is truly timeless. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2749619170456055402" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2520499967" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2520499967" />
    <a title="Humza" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18915645-humza"><img alt="Humza" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595788816p2/18915645.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2520499967?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Humza" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Humza" href="/user/show/18915645-humza">Humza</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780892590575" href="/book/show/825445.Islam_Between_East_and_West">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2520499967" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7863890165244623861">Islam Between East and West begins with the following verses from Alamma Iqbal’s Javid Nama: <br/><br/>"Though it is from the East that the sun rises,<br/>showing itself bold and bright,<br/>without a veil, it burns and blazes with inward fire<br/>only when it escapes from the shackles of East and West. <br/>Drunk with the splendor it springs up out of its East<br/>that it may subject all horizons to its mastery, <br/>its nature is innocent of both East and West, <br/>though in origin, true, it is an Easterner."<br/><br/> It seems to me that what fo</span>
  <span id="freeText7863890165244623861" style="display:none">Islam Between East and West begins with the following verses from Alamma Iqbal’s Javid Nama: <br /><br />"Though it is from the East that the sun rises,<br />showing itself bold and bright,<br />without a veil, it burns and blazes with inward fire<br />only when it escapes from the shackles of East and West. <br />Drunk with the splendor it springs up out of its East<br />that it may subject all horizons to its mastery, <br />its nature is innocent of both East and West, <br />though in origin, true, it is an Easterner."<br /><br /> It seems to me that what follows in the remainder of the book is an elaboration of the meaning of these words. Throughout “Islam between East and West”, I found myself thinking back to Iqbal’s “Reconstruction of Religious Thought” because of the very evident influence of this work had on the author. Both crafted responses that aimed to satisfy the curiosity of the Westerner and settle the faith of the Muslim. This book brought down to earth long abstracted concepts such as time and space in the Islamic cosmology I had wrestled to grasp. Having led an extremely rich and interesting life himself, Izetbegović takes the reader on a journey through western literature, art, history, politics, and even science to show how all are ultimately manifestations of man’s yearning for ultimate truth. Particularly valuable is his “Table of Opposites” in the appendix. Here he divides worldviews into the “religious,” “Islamic,” and “materialistic.” Izetbegović elaborates on each of these broad categorizations in chapters such as “Drama vs Utopia,” “City vs Countryside,” and “Prayer vs Production.” Referencing everything from Dostoevsky to Renaissance sculptures, Izetbegović explores the worlds of western intellectual/religious life and Islam and ultimately decides upon them being simultaneously distinct, yet united (along with all traditions) in shared aspiration for truth.<br /><br /> In one my favorite passages, he skillfully traces Darwinian evolution’s impact upon society and belief within the context of the aforementioned schemas. After presenting the opposing worldviews of “pure religion” and “pure materialism” and demonstrating that they are essentially negatives of one another, Islam is presented as a “bipolar unity” that contains aspects of both while being neither. This section of the book nicely ties together the preceding portions and demonstrates how the Islamic worldview is not simply an average of the two extreme worldviews, but rather a synthesis in which Islam presents a reality inevitably containing some of the truths man has come to “on his own accord.” This book compelling forwards that Islam defies man-made categorization and is indeed a unique “bipolar unity.” Although the book can wade into abstraction at times, Izetbegović does an excellent job of providing real-world examples and evidence to convincingly make his case. Alongside Muhammad Asad and Iqbal, Alija Izetbegović has surely secured himself a place as an essential guide to whom anyone seeking to understand Islam in the modern age must turn.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7863890165244623861" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_607110476" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/607110476" />
    <a title="MM" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3754944-mm"><img alt="MM" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1366338554p2/3754944.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/607110476?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MM" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MM" href="/user/show/3754944-mm">MM</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780892590575" href="/book/show/825445.Islam_Between_East_and_West">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3754944-mm?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3754944-mm?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3754944-mm?shelf=christianity">christianity</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3754944-mm?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3754944-mm?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3754944-mm?shelf=islam">islam</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer607110476" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12817879375488647526">Thought-provoking and insightful, this is a book well worth reading regardless of religious background. Alija Izetbegovic is an erudite and intelligent man whose analysis of reality touches on every aspect of existence: from life to morality to art to law and back again, all focussing on the inherent dualism in humans of spirit and matter. Rather than being a cliche, run-of-the-mill "Islam and Science" book, Izetbegovic thankfully does not once try to find a so-called "scientific miracle" in the</span>
  <span id="freeText12817879375488647526" style="display:none">Thought-provoking and insightful, this is a book well worth reading regardless of religious background. Alija Izetbegovic is an erudite and intelligent man whose analysis of reality touches on every aspect of existence: from life to morality to art to law and back again, all focussing on the inherent dualism in humans of spirit and matter. Rather than being a cliche, run-of-the-mill "Islam and Science" book, Izetbegovic thankfully does not once try to find a so-called "scientific miracle" in the Qur'an, choosing rather to analyse humans based on their experiences. Life is the supreme principle in his thought process, and dualism the result. Upbringing vs drilling, culture vs civilisation, and contemplation vs thought are just some of the topics he addresses. His knowledge of philosophies and politics is amazing, and it was astounding to see the sources (usually secular) he referenced. <br /><br />The second of half of the book, dealing with Islam as the bipolar unity between "pure religion" as represented by Christianity and materialism, is slightly less impressive. While discerning and sophisticated, it seems at times to be preaching to the choir. Nevertheless, this is an important book, and I have no doubt that a second read will be even more enlightening than the first. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12817879375488647526" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2769531337" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2769531337" />
    <a title="Z." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3441634-z"><img alt="Z." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483077134p2/3441634.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2769531337?book_show_action=true">Mar 30, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Z." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Z." href="/user/show/3441634-z">Z.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789770926796" href="/book/show/8064720">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2769531337" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14127753655967821542">This work served as Alija Izetbegovic's doctoral dissertation and has become a foundational text for a deep understanding of human being mind sets. The book is a journey through different schools of thoughts, where it shows where the humanity failed and where it succeeded.<br/><br/>It's a deep understanding of leading world views (West/Capitalism/religion - East/Socialism/materialism) shows why such philosophies have failed to give human beings what they need, the whole book is meant to offer the position</span>
  <span id="freeText14127753655967821542" style="display:none">This work served as Alija Izetbegovic's doctoral dissertation and has become a foundational text for a deep understanding of human being mind sets. The book is a journey through different schools of thoughts, where it shows where the humanity failed and where it succeeded.<br /><br />It's a deep understanding of leading world views (West/Capitalism/religion - East/Socialism/materialism) shows why such philosophies have failed to give human beings what they need, the whole book is meant to offer the position and role of Islam in the context of this sharp ideological conflict between "East and West”.<br /><br />The author addresses intellectually the parallelism or dualism of various concepts such as Culture and Civilization, Moral and Utopia, Society and Community, Morals and Religion, rill and Upbringing, Art and Religion, Art and Science, Religion and Revolution, etc), <br /><br />It’s well known that internal and the external worlds as two parallel lines that never cross philosophically, yet both of them are existed in the human beings. By differentiating between the idealism of religion and the atheism of materialism Izetbegovic argues that religion and materialism, are two poles which only Islam can reconcile and bring about their synthesis. <br /><br />He explains how materialism claims that humans are not free and only subjects to their environment and natural development and cannot be equal from that perspective (always stresses what is common to animals and humans). On the other hand religion stresses what makes them different; for example the concepts of freedom, dignity, equality and responsibility can only come from the religious perspective.<br /><br />If we understand Izetbegovit well, religion/Christianity is true but only half-way; materialism/Socialism is also true but also half-way, where as Islam being a synthesis of religion and materialism and the unity of "religion and materialism”.<br /><br />In terms of his behaviour and existential witness, Izetbegovic remained faithful to most of the ideas and views expressed in this work.<br /><br />The thing I liked most about this book is that it proofs the fact that it has always been _ possible in Islamic history to offer a provocative and an intellectual answer to the call of Islamic universalism even when the - author is not familiar with traditional Islamic sciences! Alija Izetbegovit did not study Islam formally or systematically, nor-as already mentioned-did he know Arabic. He was just hard-working and self-taught person.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14127753655967821542" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
